  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_20ad6e43-a0c6-45ae-8ead-e91188e2c0bc'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText14482644333504942518" style="display:none">With help from a genie, young Karl Anders Nilsson travels by day and night, beyond the stars, to reach Farawayland. There, his father the King, who has been searching for him for nine long years, tells him his true name is Mio, and lavishes upon him the loving attention he never received from his foster parents back in Stockholm. Mio learns of a prophecy that has been foretold for thousand of years. With his best friend Pompoo, and his horse with the golden mane, Miramis, he must travel into the darkness of Outer Land to battle the cruel Sir Kato.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=astrid-lindgren">astrid-lindgren</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=havanas-en-masse">havanas-en-masse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=sparkling">sparkling</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer981194049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16991151010426022238">All you grown-ups out there, can you hear me?<br/><br/>Yes, I mean you, literary experts, analysing the magic of children's literature until it withers and dies under your scrutiny!<br/><br/>You remember Bo Vilhelm Olsson, the boy who sat in Tegnérlunden and felt lonely, and who was then swept away on the most amazing adventure ever, where he got to meet his father and to save the children from a man with a heart of stone (literally, not figuratively made of stone)?<br/><br/>That was by far my favourite story when I was a ch</span>
  <span id="freeText16991151010426022238" style="display:none">All you grown-ups out there, can you hear me?<br /><br />Yes, I mean you, literary experts, analysing the magic of children's literature until it withers and dies under your scrutiny!<br /><br />You remember Bo Vilhelm Olsson, the boy who sat in Tegnérlunden and felt lonely, and who was then swept away on the most amazing adventure ever, where he got to meet his father and to save the children from a man with a heart of stone (literally, not figuratively made of stone)?<br /><br />That was by far my favourite story when I was a child, one of the first I read on my own, over and over, tasting the flavour of the fairytale language mixed with the reflection on the hardship of being an orphan in Stockholm city, enjoying the soothing repetition of patterns as if it was a poem.<br /><br />I read it to my own children too, feeling EXACTLY the same power flowing from the pages when my (then) primary school children cuddled up around me and took in the adventure with silent awe.<br /><br />And now, at least thirty years after my first reading, and ten after my most recent reading of Mio, Min Mio, I happen to stumble upon a literary analysis that makes my illusion break down like a house of cards. <br /><br />In the typical language (which I am guilty of myself in my incarnation as a professional) which tends to kill any spirit of any book by adding too much buzzwording and footnoting and too little understanding of what actually constitutes the joy of reading, I find a paragraph referring to "the grown-up reader" next to the child, who "knows it is all an illusion", who "knows that Bo Vilhelm Olsson is still sitting in Tegnérlunden", and that "the adventure only takes place in his head" - that it is all not magical at all. <br /><br />If that is the way I am supposed to "read" the story as a grown-up, I am quite pleased to stay a child.<br /><br />Bo Vilhelm Olsson doesn't sit there. He is Mio now, I say! And I think Astrid Lindgren thought the same. Otherwise we could just as well claim Harry never left for Hogwarts...</span>
  <a data-text-id="16991151010426022238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781590178706" href="/book/show/22926682-mio-my-son">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to mark by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/237469-mir">Mir</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=kidworld">kidworld</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2405791659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3202198530170487157"><i>Mio, my son</i>... such a kind and unassuming phrase, for such a kind and unassuming boy, in such a sweet story. Such a sweet story, full of longing and sadness - but only at first. At first there is sadness, a lack of love - but then comes happiness, an abundance of love. An abundance of love - but one tinged with a strange fear, a terror of the unknown. A terror of the unknown transformed: turned into a quest, a rescue mission. A rescue mission with a boy and his friend, both still afraid and pron</span>
  <span id="freeText3202198530170487157" style="display:none"><i>Mio, my son</i>... such a kind and unassuming phrase, for such a kind and unassuming boy, in such a sweet story. Such a sweet story, full of longing and sadness - but only at first. At first there is sadness, a lack of love - but then comes happiness, an abundance of love. An abundance of love - but one tinged with a strange fear, a terror of the unknown. A terror of the unknown transformed: turned into a quest, a rescue mission. A rescue mission with a boy and his friend, both still afraid and prone to tears; brave boys and then not-so-brave boys, terrified in the face of a great evil. A great evil, surely - but one with a core of self-loathing: sometimes even evil cannot abide itself, so the author makes clear. The author makes clear that it is love, love most of all, that little boys and little girls and all things need. All things need love: the love between friends; the love of a neighbor; love expressed through gestures and deeds, on the face and in the eyes; simple, friendly, kindly love - the love of all things, every thing, every person, each a possible new friend; the love of a parent, saying a simple, loving phrase: <i>Mio, my son...</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3202198530170487157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lynette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4364138-lynette"><img alt="Lynette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285973946p2/4364138.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/283520028?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynette" href="/user/show/4364138-lynette">Lynette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4364138-lynette?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer283520028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14562928520322034758">Maybe I'm biased because I loved the movie when I was a kid. Maybe the translation that I read is really bad. Or maybe I'm just way too old for a book written for such young kids. Whatever the reason, I really didn't understand what was so great about this book, and I'm disappointed. Ever since I was a kid, I've been looking for this book, and never could order it or anything. I remembered it a little while ago, and was able to order it on Amazon.<br/><br/>It felt so flat to me. I felt no connection to Mi</span>
  <span id="freeText14562928520322034758" style="display:none">Maybe I'm biased because I loved the movie when I was a kid. Maybe the translation that I read is really bad. Or maybe I'm just way too old for a book written for such young kids. Whatever the reason, I really didn't understand what was so great about this book, and I'm disappointed. Ever since I was a kid, I've been looking for this book, and never could order it or anything. I remembered it a little while ago, and was able to order it on Amazon.<br /><br />It felt so flat to me. I felt no connection to Mio. The story goes on so quickly that one barely has time to figure out what's going on before it rushes on to the next thing. I understand it's written for young children, but there is no build-up, the climax is rather weak, and the ending is rather flat as well. I actually found that I didn't like Mio at all. I didn't really care what happened to him. He didn't actually have to do anything for himself. When he and Pompoo (seriously, why did they change his name to Pompoo?) are being chased by spies, a tree just opens up to hide them, then the ground opens up to hide them. Then they somehow end up back at Eno's place, but the entrance to the Deepest Cave in the Blackest Mountain is right there. If it was so close to Eno's cottage, why didn't he say, "go out and hang a left"? Even when he fights Kato, Kato almost immediately just says, "kill me!" Mio works for nothing.<br /><br />Again, I understand that a young children's book isn't going to be very deep, but I at least expect it to be more interesting and coherent. And for there to actually be emotion. The horses had more emotion than the humans!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14562928520322034758" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer886349756885169418">As a child I read a lot Astrid Lindgren’s books and loved the world of imagination these stories helped me created. However, Mio, My Mio was not among them. Now I read it for the first time with my children and enjoyed it, although much less than my 7 yo. He was very excited and emotional about the story of emotionally abused Bosse who thought he had no one in his life and then discovered than in another world he was a son of a king who loved him a lot. Also, Mio had destiny to fulfil and his pa</span>
  <span id="freeText886349756885169418" style="display:none">As a child I read a lot Astrid Lindgren’s books and loved the world of imagination these stories helped me created. However, Mio, My Mio was not among them. Now I read it for the first time with my children and enjoyed it, although much less than my 7 yo. He was very excited and emotional about the story of emotionally abused Bosse who thought he had no one in his life and then discovered than in another world he was a son of a king who loved him a lot. Also, Mio had destiny to fulfil and his path was not only the journey to fight the evil but also find courage in himself.<br /><br />To be honest I couldn’t unsee the book as a story of dealing with trauma, which was terribly sad and I was scared that in the end Bosse would wake up on his bench, cold and lonely. Fortunately, it’s a book for children and it was uplifting and moving. And I loved the very title, the phrase filled with so much love it literally brought tears to my eyes.<br /><br />I really think all kids will find something for themselves in Lindgren’s books (although my son asked me to stop reading Children of Bullerbyn…) <br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer642000977424651551">Deceptively simple and straight-forward; I sense depths that I'll better plumb upon a reread in a year or two. If nothing else I'll remember Sir Kato's stone heart for a long time....<br/><br/>The only reason this might be difficult for a modern American child is that the setup takes very little time, the introduction to the land of Faraway quite a bit more, and the adventure only half the book.<br/><br/>Lots of illustrations of little boys with bare long legs and long jerseys and horses, flowers, a cottage, a well</span>
  <span id="freeText642000977424651551" style="display:none">Deceptively simple and straight-forward; I sense depths that I'll better plumb upon a reread in a year or two. If nothing else I'll remember Sir Kato's stone heart for a long time....<br /><br />The only reason this might be difficult for a modern American child is that the setup takes very little time, the introduction to the land of Faraway quite a bit more, and the adventure only half the book.<br /><br />Lots of illustrations of little boys with bare long legs and long jerseys and horses, flowers, a cottage, a well, a grandma.... Wikland used a more casual line than he did in the Lotta books, but the pictures still charm.<br /><br />Reread. Opinion holds. Beautiful story, obviously more mysterious and allegorical than a superficial reading reveals... but darned if I can lay a finger on exactly what it is that's going on under the surface. I bet it's even better in the original Swedish.</span>

<div id="review_1799779218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9783789106910" href="/book/show/1574570.Mio_mein_Mio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402776-the-bursting-bookshelf-of-a-wallflower?shelf=childhood">childhood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402776-the-bursting-bookshelf-of-a-wallflower?shelf=scandinavian">scandinavian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402776-the-bursting-bookshelf-of-a-wallflower?shelf=2016">2016</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1799779218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11178825879319734015"><b>4 stars!</b><br/><br/><b>4 stars!</b><br/><br/><i>Mio, mein Mio</i> has been one of the few <i>Astrid Lindgren</i> stories that I haven’t been familiar with during my childhood. I never read the story and I haven’t seen any movie adaptations - if they exist. I was curious to read <i>Mio, mein Mio</i>, considering that it’s one of Lindgren’s <b>most famous stories</b>. <br/><br/><i>Mio, mein Mio</i> felt a little different that most of the other stories by Lindgren. <b>Mio</b> is having a rather sad childhood. He is growing up in a foster family, which actually doesn’t like boy</span>
  <span id="freeText11178825879319734015" style="display:none"><b>4 stars!</b><br /><br /><b>4 stars!</b><br /><br /><i>Mio, mein Mio</i> has been one of the few <i>Astrid Lindgren</i> stories that I haven’t been familiar with during my childhood. I never read the story and I haven’t seen any movie adaptations - if they exist. I was curious to read <i>Mio, mein Mio</i>, considering that it’s one of Lindgren’s <b>most famous stories</b>. <br /><br /><i>Mio, mein Mio</i> felt a little different that most of the other stories by Lindgren. <b>Mio</b> is having a rather sad childhood. He is growing up in a foster family, which actually doesn’t like boys and is thus not very welcoming towards Mio. When he suddenly gets the possibility to travel to another world, far away, Mio is finding out that his <b>true father is the king of this new world</b>. For the first time in his life, Mio is feeling loved and he is fast in making new friend. However, there seems to be a dark shadow hanging over his father’s kingdom. <b>Knight Kato</b> is terrifying the country and it’s Mio’s destiny to defeat the dangerous Knight. <br /><br /><i>Mio, mein Mio</i> is a rather serious story and it made me feel <b>melancholic</b> a few times. Its message is clear and I enjoyed the characters and their courage. The <b>illustrations</b> are charming in their simplicity and made the story even stronger. I’m definitely going to read the other Lindgren stories soon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11178825879319734015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_150608020" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150608020" />
    <a title="Riana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5032961-riana"><img alt="Riana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298585452p2/5032961.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150608020?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2011</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer150608020" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10545774594924283461">if you have not read this - read it. <br/><br/>it is such a beautiful, heartwarming, dramatic, and sad story about courage and hope, about the good standing up against evil. <br/><br/>the style is simple enough to be accessible for children, but poetic enough to be enjoyed by adults. and i even think that this is one of few books by astrid lindgren that can be appreciated more by adults than children. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13808759337804527797"><blockquote>
<img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478994756i/21137858.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>"All I know is that I laughed because it made my father the king happy."<br/>
</blockquote><br/>I couldn't say what or that anything was wrong with it, but I could not connect with this book as well as I did with <b>The Brothers Lionheart</b> or <b>Ronia the Robber's Daughter</b>. It is a cute story, and although a lot of it seemed heartfelt, it struck me as distant. <br/><br/>However, I can absolutely see younger readers loving this. A lot of it seemed dream-like, some parts reminded me of fairy tales. Perhaps it is because Mio himself oft</span>

<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1478994756i/21137858.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>"All I know is that I laughed because it made my father the king happy."<br>
</blockquote><br>I couldn't say what or that anything was wrong with it, but I could not connect with this book as well as I did with <b>The Brothers Lionheart</b> or <b>Ronia the Robber's Daughter</b>. It is a cute story, and although a lot of it seemed heartfelt, it struck me as distant. <br><br>However, I can absolutely see younger readers loving this. A lot of it seemed dream-like, some parts reminded me of fairy tales. Perhaps it is because Mio himself often compared things to fairy tales, and the narration had a repetitive theme which also made it seem more like a tale than a story one gets invested into - perhaps that's why it struck me as distant?<br><br>The final paragraph seemed a bit odd <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I can't help but feel that there was a hint that all of Mio's adventures were just wishful thinking and that he, in fact, never left his unhappy life with his adoptive parents. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

      calliope

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122647-sarah-sammis?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122647-sarah-sammis?shelf=borrowed">borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122647-sarah-sammis?shelf=exwishlist">exwishlist</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102811210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12703353878539096386">Sometimes my book reviews require some story telling as set up. This is one of these posts. Scroll down if you don't want to wallow in my nostalgia.<br/><br/>When I was a child I wanted to be Pipi Longstocking's next door neighbor. I figured I wasn't cool enough or strong enough to live on my own, eat nails and carry a horse with my bare hands but I sure wanted a friend who could! I read everyone of Astrid Lindgren's Pipi books and watched all the poorly dubbed movies more times than I can count.<br/><br/>But someh</span>
  <span id="freeText12703353878539096386" style="display:none">Sometimes my book reviews require some story telling as set up. This is one of these posts. Scroll down if you don't want to wallow in my nostalgia.<br /><br />When I was a child I wanted to be Pipi Longstocking's next door neighbor. I figured I wasn't cool enough or strong enough to live on my own, eat nails and carry a horse with my bare hands but I sure wanted a friend who could! I read everyone of Astrid Lindgren's Pipi books and watched all the poorly dubbed movies more times than I can count.<br /><br />But somehow I never wondered if Astrid Lindgren had ever written anything else. That is until I was in graduate school and one of my classmates was Swedish. She was a huge fan of Lindgren and had grown up reading all of her books.<br /><br />Now it's 15 years later and I'm finally making the effort to read her other books.<br /><br />The first one I picked was Mio, My Son. It begins like so many children's fantasy novels do, with an orphan, Karl Anders Nilsson, wondering about his real parents. Karl, like, Pipi, isn't really an orphan; he's just been separated from his father by extraordinary circumstances.<br /><br />Then one day he gets the chance to be reunited, being taken to Farawayland by a genie. There the boy learns his name is actually Mio and he settles into his new life.<br /><br />If had one complaint about Mio, My Son, is the ease at which he adapts to a new life, a new (to him) father, new duties and a new name. But, that seems to be Lindgren's style. Pipi's adventures are always rather matter-of-fact. There's no logical reason for me to expect different rules or behavior for Mio's world than Pipi's and yet part of me does.<br /><br />That said, it's a quick read with lots of heart. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12703353878539096386" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1790133578" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1790133578" />
    <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all"><img alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1790133578?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=thinking-people-s-books">thinking-people-s-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1790133578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8820614642410563568">A very good bedtime read. The story of unwanted adoptee Andy, who discovers he is actually the son of the king of Farawayland. He is taken there and enjoys learning about his true home, but it comes at a price--he must face the evil Sir Kato!<br/><br/>Wonderful illustrations, and the tale resonates strongly with Norse myth and legend (hardly surprising, given that Lindgren was Swedish.) However, there are a few quibbles about the lack of development of some promising threads. Mio's best friend Pompoo repe</span>
  <span id="freeText8820614642410563568" style="display:none">A very good bedtime read. The story of unwanted adoptee Andy, who discovers he is actually the son of the king of Farawayland. He is taken there and enjoys learning about his true home, but it comes at a price--he must face the evil Sir Kato!<br /><br />Wonderful illustrations, and the tale resonates strongly with Norse myth and legend (hardly surprising, given that Lindgren was Swedish.) However, there are a few quibbles about the lack of development of some promising threads. Mio's best friend Pompoo repeatedly tells him "There's a lot you don't know, Mio." Well, that can be said of anyone...but I kept wondering why Pompoo doesn't help him find out some stuff that Pompoo obviously <i>does</i> know and Mio doesn't. I was also intrigued that there is no real explanation of where Kato came from...a paragraph or two would do it.<br /><br />The repetition of the line "I was scared. So scared." and Pompoo's lament about being so small, so defenseless and so alone reminded me of a friend's definition of courage: it doesn't mean not feeling fear, it means doing it scared. A valuable lesson for children and adults alike.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8820614642410563568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer606290034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11978688593596658820">Karl Anders Nilson, known as Andy, is a lonely boy. An orphan, he lives in Stockholm with his foster parents, Aunt Hulda who wanted a boy and Uncle Olaf who thinks Andy makes too much noise. His only friend is Ben, but they sometimes fight. The only person who is kind to him is Mrs Lundy from the sweet shop. But then one day Aunt Hulda, after telling Andy yet again that the day he came to their house was an unlucky one, is given a card to post by Mrs Lundy and an apple that turns to gold. The ca</span>
  <span id="freeText11978688593596658820" style="display:none">Karl Anders Nilson, known as Andy, is a lonely boy. An orphan, he lives in Stockholm with his foster parents, Aunt Hulda who wanted a boy and Uncle Olaf who thinks Andy makes too much noise. His only friend is Ben, but they sometimes fight. The only person who is kind to him is Mrs Lundy from the sweet shop. But then one day Aunt Hulda, after telling Andy yet again that the day he came to their house was an unlucky one, is given a card to post by Mrs Lundy and an apple that turns to gold. The card is to the King of Farawayland. Sitting in Tegnerlunden Park watching Ben eat with his loving parents through their lighted window he cries, but on the ground is a bottle and in the bottle is a genie. For release from the bottle he grants Andy one wish, and he wishes to go to Farawayland. <br /><br />A magical adventure of lost princes, love, friendship, courage, a white horse named Miramis, a bridge called Morninglight and a terrible foe, Sir Kato, begins. Andy learns the truth of his birth and finds both happiness and a strength he never new he had.<br /><br />Simply one of the most beautiful enchanting stories I have ever read. I read this aloud over several days and nights to my 9 year old and enjoyed every moment. Written in mesmeric prose, in less skillful hands this would have been just a good story to tell a younger child, but Lindgren makes it magical, mythic and unsentimental.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11978688593596658820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10034132775480544597">This story contains some folk essence and Lindgren's imagination. Beautiful fantasy and I was about to cry when I found how the king, Mio's father loves his son. Ending was also good.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3567501003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3567501003" />
    <a title="Jimmy Brandt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/97960925-jimmy-brandt"><img alt="Jimmy Brandt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604936413p2/97960925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3567501003?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy Brandt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy Brandt" href="/user/show/97960925-jimmy-brandt">Jimmy Brandt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789129678154" href="/book/show/17237769-mio-min-mio">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3567501003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3201879341084672294">A swedish childrens classic about a boy who travels to a land far away to help get captured children back from the evil Kato!<br/><br/>I might be judging it too hard with my 3 stars - but I try to give it a fair opinion about how I felt when reading it now as an adult, the rest of Astrid Lindgrens legacy set aside. I wanted more details to this story, a lot more details but at the same time I know it's a childrens book from the 1950s so I can't expect it to be as fleshed out as an adult fantasy book.<br/><br/>What </span>
  <span id="freeText3201879341084672294" style="display:none">A swedish childrens classic about a boy who travels to a land far away to help get captured children back from the evil Kato!<br /><br />I might be judging it too hard with my 3 stars - but I try to give it a fair opinion about how I felt when reading it now as an adult, the rest of Astrid Lindgrens legacy set aside. I wanted more details to this story, a lot more details but at the same time I know it's a childrens book from the 1950s so I can't expect it to be as fleshed out as an adult fantasy book.<br /><br />What made me feel a bit disconnected to the story and the characters is the fact that there is a ton of unexplained things... a lot. A LOT. Also Kato just being a plain 'evil guy sit and think evil things so evil things happen' felt like the defintion of being one dimentional; even for a kids story.<br />The unexplained questions made me feel a bit unsatisfied after I turned the last page. I waited for something to tie the story together in the end but no. But I do wish I had read this as a child - as that's the target audience. <br /><br />I want to love it, but I do like it - even after my harsh words above. After all 3 stars does not mean it's bad, but I can't give it a higher score after getting the feeling of being unsatisfied after finishing it. I do recommend reading it to children if you want to prepare them moving into fantasy books with more serious themes and concepts - this does contain some dark moments, but it's not too intense. As an adult not having read it as a kid, you won't really have missed anything if you skip this one - even if it's a part of the swedish litterary canon.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1521978961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1521978961" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50448-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175789489p2/50448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1521978961?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/50448-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781590178706" href="/book/show/22926682-mio-my-son">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50448-becky?shelf=2016reviews">2016reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50448-becky?shelf=librarybook">librarybook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50448-becky?shelf=2016vtchallenge">2016vtchallenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1521978961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7144693192224066516">Here's how Mio, My Son begins: "Did you listen to the radio on October 15th last year? Did you hear the news about a boy who disappeared? This is what it said: 'Police in Stockholm are searching for a nine-year-old boy missing from his home, at 13 North Street, since 6.P.M. two days ago. Karl Anders Nilsson has light hair and blue eyes. At the time of his disappearance he was wearing brown shorts, a gray sweater, and a small red cap. Anyone with more information on his whereabouts should contact</span>
  <span id="freeText7144693192224066516" style="display:none">Here's how Mio, My Son begins: "Did you listen to the radio on October 15th last year? Did you hear the news about a boy who disappeared? This is what it said: 'Police in Stockholm are searching for a nine-year-old boy missing from his home, at 13 North Street, since 6.P.M. two days ago. Karl Anders Nilsson has light hair and blue eyes. At the time of his disappearance he was wearing brown shorts, a gray sweater, and a small red cap. Anyone with more information on his whereabouts should contact the police.'"<br /><br />I don't even know why, but, something about that opening paragraph grabbed me. I wanted to read more. I knew nothing about the book, but I knew I wanted to make time to read it. (When was the last time you got hooked into a book?! I'd love to hear about it!)<br /><br />So, you might think based on the opening paragraph that Mio, My Son was realistic fiction. That it was perhaps a bit on the dark side, and, that it would perhaps involve a kidnapping. Unless you've read reviews of it, you might not be expecting to find a FAIRY-TALE like fantasy novel set not in the 'real world' but in Farawayland. I know I was surprised--quite pleasantly--to find that Mio, My Son IS a fantasy novel.<br /><br />The hero of this one is a boy sometimes called 'Andy' but usually called MIO. He is the 'missing boy.' He is narrating his own story, and doing it in his own way. The narrative voice is quite strong, in my opinion. <br /><br />Now, I will warn readers that sometimes Mio repeats himself. For example, "I must go there to fight Sir Kato, though I was so scared, so scared." Some readers might find this an unforgivable sin. I don't. Not in this case at least. I didn't find it as annoying as a written stutter, for example. Perhaps because it mainly occurs when Mio is thinking about or talking about Sir Kato. It doesn't occur on every page. <br /><br />So essentially, the book is Mio's adventures in Farawayland. The first half of the book is mostly light and joyous. Nothing heavy or dark. The second half of the book, however, is much more dramatic and dark. THINK Lord of the Rings only for a much younger audience. Mio has a mission to accomplish, something that only HE, as a royal son, can do. And it is seemingly impossible and very daunting. Mio must make up his mind to be brave and determined and risk everything for his mission. <br /><br />Mio is not alone. He has a best friend, Pompoo, and a horse, Miramis. And, there is, of course, his father THE KING, who I personally LOVED. <br /><br />So did I like this one? Did I love it? Did I love, love, LOVE it? I think I definitely loved it. I loved it for the narrative, for the descriptive language, for the imagery. I really loved the imagery of the Bread That Satisfies Hunger and the Well That Quenches Thirst. Also I really liked the Well That Whispers at Night. The first two images reminded me of Scripture. (John 4:13-14; John 6:35) The sacrificial nature of the mission also reminded me of Scripture. I'm not convinced it can only, always be read as an "allegory" (think The Lion, The Witch, and the Wardrobe). But as a Christian reader, I saw how it could be interpreted that way. <br /><br />I think anyone can appreciate the imagery of the Well That Whispers At Night:<br /><blockquote>A whisper began deep, deep down in the well. It was such a strange voice, unlike any other voice. It whispered fairy tales. They weren't like any other fairy tales, and they were the most beautiful stories in the whole world. There was almost nothing that I loved more than listening to fairy tales, so I lay down flat on my stomach, leaning over the edge of the well to hear more and more of the voice that whispered. Sometimes it sang too, the strangest and most beautiful songs. <br />"What strange kind of well is this?" I said to Totty.<br />"A well full of fairy tales and songs. That's all I know," said Totty. "A well full of old stories and songs that have existed in the world for a long time, but that people forgot a long time ago. It is only the Well That Whispers at Night that remembers them all."</blockquote> <br /><br /> Here's another favorite passage:<br /><blockquote>I understood then for the first time that I never needed to be afraid of my father the King, that whatever I did he would always look at me kindly, like he was doing now as he stood there with his hand on the Master Rose Gardener's shoulder and with all the white birds flying around him. And when I understood him, I was happier than I'd ever been before in my life. I was so glad that I laughed quite hard.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="7144693192224066516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1312889753?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Storywraps" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Storywraps" href="/user/show/5292218-storywraps">Storywraps</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781590178706" href="/book/show/22926682-mio-my-son">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1312889753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6223790102294730373">Mio, My Son is a children's book by Swedish writer Astrid Lindgren. It was first published in 1954 in Sweden, with the Swedish title Mio, min Mio. The writing is stylised and the story strongly reminiscent of traditional fairy tales and folklore. Wikipedia<br/>Published: 1954<br/>Adaptations: Mio in the Land of Faraway (1987)<br/><br/><br/>Nine-year-old Karl Anders Nilsson is an orphaned boy who is fostered by a mean couple who really wanted a girl but settled for him. His mother died at birth and nobody even bothered t</span>
  <span id="freeText6223790102294730373" style="display:none">Mio, My Son is a children's book by Swedish writer Astrid Lindgren. It was first published in 1954 in Sweden, with the Swedish title Mio, min Mio. The writing is stylised and the story strongly reminiscent of traditional fairy tales and folklore. Wikipedia<br />Published: 1954<br />Adaptations: Mio in the Land of Faraway (1987)<br /><br /><br />Nine-year-old Karl Anders Nilsson is an orphaned boy who is fostered by a mean couple who really wanted a girl but settled for him. His mother died at birth and nobody even bothered to trace where his father may be so he was placed in an orphanage. Poor Karl is treated very poorly by the couple and he feels ever so lonely and unloved. He yearns to find his real father and have a relationship with him like his best friend Ben has with his father....someone that will care and cherish him dearly. <br /><br />Then, on October 15th, he simply disappears, without a trace, right off the face of the earth...literally. How could that possibly happen? Feeling very sad and downtrodden that day, while sitting on a park bench beside himself with sadness, he encounters a genie in a bottle, and once released Karl is granted one wish. He wishes to locate his dad and instantly he finds himself speeding to a land far, far away called, FarawayLand. He meets his dad who is the beloved King there and a very happy reunion ensues. Karl find out he is really Mio and a dangerous challenge faces him, one that can only be successful and carried out by someone in the royal bloodline. Prophesies have declared his coming for thousands of years and the mission he is to carry out is very dangerous indeed. <br /><br />He acquires a new friend, Pompoo, and a magical flying horse with a golden mane named, Miramis, to help him conquer evil Sir Kato, the cruel abductor of children of in Farawayland. Can a small, untrained little boy face such a force of evil and destroy him restoring happiness and tranquility back to his father's kingdom? Will Mio be brave enough to even try? This story will have you reading intently right up to the very last page. I highly, highly recommend this book. Fairytales do come true ...<br /><br /><br />AND THEY LIVED HAPPILY EVER AFTER!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6223790102294730373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }

      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''

<span id="freeTextContainer8827585253267723474">Astrid Anna Emilia Lindgren, née Ericsson, (1907 - 2002) was a Swedish children's book author and screenwriter, whose many titles were translated into 85 languages and published in more than 100 countries. She has sold roughly 165 million copies worldwide. Today, she is most remembered for writing the <i>Pippi Longstocking</i> books, as well as the <i>Karlsson-on-the-Roof</i> book series.<br/><br/>Awards:<br/>Hans Christian A</span>
  <span id="freeText8827585253267723474" style="display:none">Astrid Anna Emilia Lindgren, née Ericsson, (1907 - 2002) was a Swedish children's book author and screenwriter, whose many titles were translated into 85 languages and published in more than 100 countries. She has sold roughly 165 million copies worldwide. Today, she is most remembered for writing the <i>Pippi Longstocking</i> books, as well as the <i>Karlsson-on-the-Roof</i> book series.<br /><br />Awards:<br />Hans Christian Andersen Award for Writing (1958)</span>
